<p>The <b>Eiffel Tower</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/aɪ/ long 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aʊər/ 'our' in 'hour'">aʊər</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key" class="mw-redirect"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">EYE</span></span></b>-fəl</i></a></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key" class="mw-redirect"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">TOWR</span></span></b></i></a></span>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><b>tour Eiffel</b></i></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[tuʁ‿ɛfɛl]</a></span> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_Eiffel_Pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Tour_Eiffel_Pronunciation.ogg" class="internal" title="Tour Eiffel Pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> <a href="/wiki/Lattice_tower" title="Lattice tower">lattice tower</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Champ_de_Mars" title="Champ de Mars">Champ de Mars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, France. It is named after the engineer <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Eiffel" title="Gustave Eiffel">Gustave Eiffel</a>, whose company designed and built the tower. Constructed in 1889 as the entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1889)" title="Exposition Universelle (1889)">1889 World's Fair</a>, it was initially criticized by some of France's leading artists and intellectuals for its design, but has become a global <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">cultural icon</a> of France and one of the most recognisable structures in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-things_to_remember_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-things_to_remember-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The tower is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures_in_the_Paris_region" title="List of tallest buildings and structures in the Paris region">tallest structure in Paris</a> and the most-visited paid monument in the world: 6.98 million people ascended it in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The tower received its 250 millionth visitor in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The tower is 324 metres (1,063&#160;ft) tall,<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> about the same height as an 81-storey building. Its base is square, 125 metres (410&#160;ft) on a side. During its construction, the Eiffel Tower surpassed the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world#Timeline_of_world.27s_tallest_freestanding_structures" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">tallest man-made structure</a> in the world, a title it held for 41 years until the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> was built in 1930. Due to the addition of the <a href="/wiki/Aerial_(radio)" title="Aerial (radio)" class="mw-redirect">aerial</a> at the top of the tower in 1957, it is now taller than the Chrysler Building by 5.2 metres (17&#160;ft). Not including broadcast aerials, it is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_structures_in_France" title="List of tallest structures in France">second-tallest structure in France</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Millau_Viaduct" title="Millau Viaduct">Millau Viaduct</a>.</p>
<p>The tower has three levels for visitors, with restaurants on the first and second. The top level's upper platform is 276&#160;m (906&#160;ft) above the ground,<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> the highest accessible to the public in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. Tickets can be purchased to ascend by stairs or lift (elevator) to the first and second levels. The climb from ground level to the first level is over 300 steps, as is the climb from the first level to the second. Although there is a staircase to the top level, it is usually only accessible by lift.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg/170px-Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg" width="170" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg/255px-Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg/340px-Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="1086" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maurice_koechlin_pylone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First drawing of the Eiffel Tower by Maurice Koechlin including size comparison with other Parisian landmarks such as <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a>, the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_aux_Cygnes#Statue_of_Liberty_replica" title="Île aux Cygnes">Statue of Liberty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vend%C3%B4me_Column" title="Vendôme Column" class="mw-redirect">Vendôme Column</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The design of the Eiffel Tower was the product of <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Koechlin" title="Maurice Koechlin">Maurice Koechlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Nouguier" title="Émile Nouguier">Émile Nouguier</a>, two senior engineers working for the <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Eiffel" title="Gustave Eiffel">Compagnie des Établissements Eiffel</a>, after discussion about a suitable centrepiece for the proposed <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1889)" title="Exposition Universelle (1889)">1889 Exposition Universelle</a>, a <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World's fair">world's fair</a> to celebrate the centennial of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. Eiffel openly acknowledged that inspiration for a tower came from the <a href="/wiki/Latting_Observatory" title="Latting Observatory">Latting Observatory</a> built in New York City in 1853.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1884, working at home, Koechlin made a sketch of their idea, described by him as "a great <a href="/wiki/Pylon_(architecture)" title="Pylon (architecture)">pylon</a>, consisting of four lattice <a href="/wiki/Girder" title="Girder">girders</a> standing apart at the base and coming together at the top, joined together by metal trusses at regular intervals".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Eiffel initially showed little enthusiasm, but he did approve further study, and the two engineers then asked <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Sauvestre" title="Stephen Sauvestre">Stephen Sauvestre</a>, the head of company's architectural department, to contribute to the design. Sauvestre added decorative arches to the base of the tower, a glass pavilion to the first level, and other embellishments.</p>
<p>The new version gained Eiffel's support: he bought the rights to the patent on the design which Koechlin, Nougier, and Sauvestre had taken out, and the design was exhibited at the Exhibition of Decorative Arts in the autumn of 1884 under the company name. On 30 March 1885, Eiffel presented his plans to the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Société des Ingénieurs Civils</span>; after discussing the technical problems and emphasising the practical uses of the tower, he finished his talk by saying the tower would symbolise,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"Not only the art of the modern engineer, but also the century of Industry and Science in which we are living, and for which the way was prepared by the great scientific movement of the eighteenth century and by the Revolution of 1789, to which this monument will be built as an expression of France's gratitude."<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette116_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette116-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Little progress was made until 1886, when <a href="/wiki/Jules_Gr%C3%A9vy" title="Jules Grévy">Jules Grévy</a> was re-elected as president of France and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Lockroy" title="Édouard Lockroy">Édouard Lockroy</a> was appointed as minister for trade. A budget for the exposition was passed and, on 1 May, Lockroy announced an alteration to the terms of the open competition being held for a centerpiece to the exposition, which effectively made the selection of Eiffel's design a foregone conclusion, as entries had to include a study for a 300&#160;m (980&#160;ft) four-sided metal tower on the Champ de Mars.<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette116_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette116-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> On 12 May, a commission was set up to examine Eiffel's scheme and its rivals, which, a month later, decided that all the proposals except Eiffel's were either impractical or lacking in details.</p>
<p>After some debate about the exact location of the tower, a contract was signed on 8 January 1887. This was signed by Eiffel acting in his own capacity rather than as the representative of his company, and granted him 1.5 million francs toward the construction costs: less than a quarter of the estimated 6.5 million francs. Eiffel was to receive all income from the commercial exploitation of the tower during the exhibition and for the next 20 years. He later established a separate company to manage the tower, putting up half the necessary capital himself.<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette121_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette121-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_artists.27_protest">The artists' protest</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png/170px-Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png" width="170" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png 2x" data-file-width="227" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Caricature_Gustave_Eiffel.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Caricature of Gustave Eiffel comparing the Eiffel tower to the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">Pyramids</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG/170px-Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG/255px-Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG 2x" data-file-width="327" data-file-height="440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guillaume_Apollinaire_Calligramme.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Calligram" title="Calligram">calligram</a> by <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Apollinaire" title="Guillaume Apollinaire">Guillaume Apollinaire</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The proposed tower had been a subject of controversy, drawing criticism from those who did not believe it was feasible and those who objected on artistic grounds. These objections were an expression of a long-standing debate in France about the relationship between architecture and engineering. It came to a head as work began at the Champ de Mars: a "Committee of Three Hundred" (one member for each metre of the tower's height) was formed, led by the prominent architect <a href="/wiki/Charles_Garnier_(architect)" title="Charles Garnier (architect)">Charles Garnier</a> and including some of the most important figures of the arts, such as <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Bouguereau" title="Adolphe Bouguereau" class="mw-redirect">Adolphe Bouguereau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guy_de_Maupassant" title="Guy de Maupassant">Guy de Maupassant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gounod" title="Charles Gounod">Charles Gounod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Massenet" title="Jules Massenet">Jules Massenet</a>. A petition called "Artists against the Eiffel Tower" was sent to the Minister of Works and Commissioner for the Exposition, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Charles_Alphand" title="Jean-Charles Alphand">Charles Alphand</a>, and it was published by <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Temps_(Paris)" title="Le Temps (Paris)">Le Temps</a></i> on 14 February 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette174_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette174-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We, writers, painters, sculptors, architects and passionate devotees of the hitherto untouched beauty of Paris, protest with all our strength, with all our indignation in the name of slighted French taste, against the erection [...] of this useless and monstrous Eiffel Tower [...] To bring our arguments home, imagine for a moment a giddy, ridiculous tower dominating Paris like a gigantic black smokestack, crushing under its barbaric bulk <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tour_Saint-Jacques" title="Tour Saint-Jacques" class="mw-redirect">Tour Saint-Jacques</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>, the Dome of <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">les Invalides</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arc_de_Triomphe" title="Arc de Triomphe">Arc de Triomphe</a>, all of our humiliated monuments will disappear in this ghastly dream. And for twenty years [...] we shall see stretching like a blot of ink the hateful shadow of the hateful column of bolted sheet metal.</p>
</blockquote>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Gustave Eiffel responded to these criticisms by comparing his tower to the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">Egyptian pyramids</a>: "My tower will be the tallest edifice ever erected by man. Will it not also be grandiose in its way? And why would something admirable in Egypt become hideous and ridiculous in Paris?"<sup id="cite_ref-SouriauSouriau1983_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SouriauSouriau1983-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> These criticisms were also dealt with by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Lockroy" title="Édouard Lockroy">Édouard Lockroy</a> in a letter of support written to Alphand, ironically saying,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> "Judging by the stately swell of the rhythms, the beauty of the metaphors, the elegance of its delicate and precise style, one can tell this protest is the result of collaboration of the most famous writers and poets of our time", and he explained that the protest was irrelevant since the project had been decided upon months before, and construction on the tower was already under way.</p>
<p>Indeed, Garnier was a member of the Tower Commission that had examined the various proposals, and had raised no objection. Eiffel was similarly unworried, pointing out to a journalist that it was premature to judge the effect of the tower solely on the basis of the drawings, that the Champ de Mars was distant enough from the monuments mentioned in the protest for there to be little risk of the tower overwhelming them, and putting the aesthetic argument for the tower: "Do not the laws of natural forces always conform to the secret laws of harmony?"<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette176_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette176-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the protesters changed their minds when the tower was built; others remained unconvinced.<sup id="cite_ref-times1_4_89_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times1_4_89-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guy_de_Maupassant" title="Guy de Maupassant">Guy de Maupassant</a> supposedly ate lunch in the tower's restaurant every day because it was the one place in Paris where the tower was not visible.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By 1918, it had become a symbol of Paris and of France after <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Apollinaire" title="Guillaume Apollinaire">Guillaume Apollinaire</a> wrote a nationalist poem in the shape of the tower (a <a href="/wiki/Calligram" title="Calligram">calligram</a>) to express his feelings about the war against Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Apollinaire1980_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apollinaire1980-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, it is widely considered to be a remarkable piece of <a href="/wiki/Structural_art" title="Structural art">structural art</a>, and is often featured in films and literature.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel,_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg/220px-Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg/330px-Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg/440px-Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel%2C_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="377" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Les_fondations_de_la_Tour_Eiffel,_ma%C3%A7onneries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Foundation_(engineering)" title="Foundation (engineering)">Foundations</a> of the Eiffel Tower</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG/220px-Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG/330px-Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG/440px-Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="538" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Construction_tour_eiffel.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The start of the erection of the metalwork</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Work on the foundations started on 28 January 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-origins_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origins-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Those for the east and south legs were straightforward, with each leg resting on four 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) concrete slabs, one for each of the principal girders of each leg. The west and north legs, being closer to the river <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a>, were more complicated: each slab needed two <a href="/wiki/Deep_foundation" title="Deep foundation">piles</a> installed by using compressed-air <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(engineering)" title="Caisson (engineering)">caissons</a> 15&#160;m (49&#160;ft) long and 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) in diameter driven to a depth of 22&#160;m (72&#160;ft)<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette123_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette123-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> to support the concrete slabs, which were 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) thick. Each of these slabs supported a block of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> with an inclined top to bear a supporting shoe for the ironwork.</p>
<p>Each shoe was anchored to the stonework by a pair of bolts 10&#160;cm (4&#160;in) in diameter and 7.5&#160;m (25&#160;ft) long. The foundations were completed on 30 June, and the erection of the ironwork began. The visible work on-site was complemented by the enormous amount of exacting preparatory work that took place behind the scenes: the drawing office produced 1,700 general drawings and 3,629 detailed drawings of the 18,038 different parts needed.<sup id="cite_ref-loyrette148_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loyrette148-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The task of drawing the components was complicated by the complex angles involved in the design and the degree of precision required: the position of rivet holes was specified to within 0.1&#160;mm (0.004&#160;in) and angles worked out to one <a href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond" class="mw-redirect">second of arc</a>. The finished components, some already riveted together into sub-assemblies, arrived on horse-drawn carts from a factory in the nearby Parisian suburb of <a href="/wiki/Levallois-Perret" title="Levallois-Perret">Levallois-Perret</a> and were first bolted together, with the bolts being replaced with rivets as construction progressed. No drilling or shaping was done on site: if any part did not fit, it was sent back to the factory for alteration. In all, 18,038 pieces were joined together using 2.5&#160;million rivets.<sup id="cite_ref-origins_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origins-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At first the legs were constructed as <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevers</a>, but about halfway to the first level, construction was paused in order to create a substantial timber <a href="/wiki/Scaffold" title="Scaffold" class="mw-redirect">scaffold</a>. This renewed concerns about the structural integrity of the tower, and sensational headlines such as "Eiffel Suicide!" and "Gustave Eiffel Has Gone Mad: He Has Been Confined in an Asylum" appeared in the tabloid press.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> At this stage, a small "creeper" <a href="/wiki/Crane_(machine)" title="Crane (machine)">crane</a> designed to move up the tower was installed in each leg. They made use of the guides for the lifts which were to be fitted in the four legs. The critical stage of joining the legs at the first level was completed by the end of March 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-origins_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origins-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the metalwork had been prepared with the utmost attention to detail, provision had been made to carry out small adjustments in order to precisely align the legs; <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic" class="mw-redirect">hydraulic</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_(device)" title="Jack (device)">jacks</a> were fitted to the shoes at the base of each leg, capable of exerting a force of 800 tonnes, and the legs were intentionally constructed at a slightly steeper angle than necessary, being supported by <a href="/wiki/Sand_box_(civil_engineering)" title="Sand box (civil engineering)">sandboxes</a> on the scaffold. Although construction involved 300 on-site employees,<sup id="cite_ref-origins_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origins-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> only one person died thanks to Eiffel's stringent safety precautions and the use of movable gangways, guardrails and screens.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 158px">
<div style="width: 158px">

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 158px">
<div style="width: 158px">

<div style="margin:-0.16666666666666px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG/234px-Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG" width="156" height="126" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG/351px-Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG/469px-Construction_tour_eiffel3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="522" /></a></div>

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 158px">
<div style="width: 158px">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifts">Lifts</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux,_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg/220px-Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg/330px-Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg/440px-Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux%2C_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="485" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_Eiffel_Ascenseur_Roux,_Combaluzier_et_Lepape.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Roux, Combaluzier &amp; Lepape lifts during construction. Note the drive sprockets and chain in the foreground</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Equipping the tower with adequate and safe passenger lifts was a major concern of the government commission overseeing the Exposition. Although some visitors could be expected to climb to the first level, or even the second, lifts clearly had to be the main means of ascent.<sup id="cite_ref-vogel_20_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogel_20-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Constructing lifts to reach the first level was relatively straightforward: the legs were wide enough at the bottom and so nearly straight that they could contain a straight track, and a contract was given to the French company Roux, Combaluzier &amp; Lepape for two lifts to be fitted in the east and west legs.<sup id="cite_ref-vogel_28_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogel_28-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Roux, Combaluzier &amp; Lepape used a pair of endless chains with rigid, articulated links to which the car was attached. Lead weights on some links of the upper or return sections of the chains counterbalanced most of the car's weight. The car was pushed up from below, not pulled up from above: to prevent the chain buckling, it was enclosed in a conduit. At the bottom of the run, the chains passed around 3.9&#160;m (12&#160;ft 10&#160;in) diameter <a href="/wiki/Sprocket" title="Sprocket">sprockets</a>. Smaller sprockets at the top guided the chains.<sup id="cite_ref-vogel_28_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogel_28-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg/170px-Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg/255px-Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg/340px-Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="435" data-file-height="550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eiffel-Otis_lift-poyet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Otis lifts originally fitted in the north and south legs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Installing lifts to the second level was more of a challenge because a straight track was impossible. No French company wanted to undertake the work. The European branch of <a href="/wiki/Otis_Elevator_Company" title="Otis Elevator Company">Otis Brothers &amp; Company</a> submitted a proposal but this was rejected: the fair's charter ruled out the use of any foreign material in the construction of the tower. The deadline for bids was extended but still no French companies put themselves forward, and eventually the contract was given to Otis in July 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-vogel_23-4_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogel_23-4-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Otis were confident they would eventually be given the contract and had already started creating designs.</p>
<p>The car was divided into two superimposed compartments, each holding 25 passengers, with the lift operator occupying an exterior platform on the first level. <a href="/wiki/Motive_power" title="Motive power">Motive power</a> was provided by an inclined hydraulic ram 12.67&#160;m (41&#160;ft 7&#160;in) long and 96.5&#160;cm (38&#160;in) in diameter in the tower leg with a stroke of 10.83&#160;m (35&#160;ft 6&#160;in): this moved a carriage carrying six sheaves. Five fixed sheaves were mounted higher up the leg, producing an arrangement similar to a <a href="/wiki/Block_and_tackle" title="Block and tackle">block and tackle</a> but acting in reverse, multiplying the stroke of the piston rather than the force generated. The hydraulic pressure in the driving cylinder was produced by a large open reservoir on the second level. After being exhausted from the cylinder, the water was pumped back up to the reservoir by two pumps in the machinery room at the base of the south leg. This reservoir also provided power to the lifts to the first level.</p>
<p>The original lifts for the journey between the second and third levels were supplied by Léon Edoux. A pair of 81&#160;m (266&#160;ft) hydraulic rams were mounted on the second level, reaching nearly halfway up to the third level. One lift car was mounted on top of these rams: cables ran from the top of this car up to sheaves on the third level and back down to a second car. Each car only travelled half the distance between the second and third levels and passengers were required to change lifts halfway by means of a short gangway. The 10-ton cars each held 65 passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration_and_the_1889_exposition">Inauguration and the 1889 exposition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg/220px-Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg/330px-Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg/440px-Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="347" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vue_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale_de_l%27Exposition_universelle_de_1889.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General view of the Exposition Universelle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The main structural work was completed at the end of March 1889 and, on the 31st of March, Eiffel celebrated by leading a group of government officials, accompanied by representatives of the press, to the top of the tower.<sup id="cite_ref-times1_4_89_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times1_4_89-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Because the lifts were not yet in operation, the ascent was made by foot, and took over an hour, with Eiffel stopping frequently to explain various features. Most of the party chose to stop at the lower levels, but a few, including the structural engineer, Émile Nouguier, the head of construction, Jean Compagnon, the President of the City Council, and reporters from <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Figaro" title="Le Figaro">Le Figaro</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Monde_Illustr%C3%A9" title="Le Monde Illustré" class="mw-redirect">Le Monde Illustré</a></i>, completed the ascent. At 2:35&#160;pm, Eiffel hoisted a large <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_France" title="Flag of France">Tricolour</a> to the accompaniment of a 25-gun salute fired at the first level.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There was still work to be done, particularly on the lifts and facilities, and the tower was not opened to the public until nine days after the opening of the exposition on 6 May; even then, the lifts had not been completed. The tower was an instant success with the public, and nearly 30,000 visitors made the 1,710-step climb to the top before the lifts entered service on 26 May.<sup id="cite_ref-Exposition_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exposition-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Tickets cost 2 francs for the first level, 3 for the second, and 5 for the top, with half-price admission on Sundays,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> and by the end of the exhibition there had been 1,896,987 visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After dark, the tower was lit by hundreds of gas lamps, and a beacon sent out three beams of red, white and blue light. Two searchlights mounted on a circular rail were used to illuminate various buildings of the exposition. The daily opening and closing of the exposition were announced by a cannon at the top.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg/170px-Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg" width="170" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg/255px-Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg/340px-Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="894" data-file-height="1400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Georges_Garen_embrasement_tour_Eiffel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illumination of the tower at night during the exposition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the second level, the French newspaper <i>Le Figaro</i> had an office and a printing press, where a special souvenir edition, <i>Le Figaro de la Tour</i>, was made. There was also a <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A2tisserie" title="Pâtisserie">pâtisserie</a>.</p>
<p>At the top, there was a post office where visitors could send letters and postcards as a memento of their visit. <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">Graffitists</a> were also catered for: sheets of paper were mounted on the walls each day for visitors to record their impressions of the tower. Gustave Eiffel described some of the responses as <i>vraiment curieuse</i> ("truly curious").<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Famous visitors to the tower included the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Prince of Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Bernhardt" title="Sarah Bernhardt">Sarah Bernhardt</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Cody" title="William Cody" class="mw-redirect">"Buffalo Bill" Cody</a> (his Wild West show was an attraction at the exposition) and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Exposition_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exposition-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Eiffel invited Edison to his private apartment at the top of the tower, where Edison presented him with one of his <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonographs</a>, a new invention and one of the many highlights of the exposition.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Edison signed the <a href="/wiki/Guestbook" title="Guestbook">guestbook</a> with this message:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>To M Eiffel the Engineer the brave builder of so gigantic and original specimen of modern Engineering from one who has the greatest respect and admiration for all Engineers including the Great Engineer the Bon Dieu, Thomas Edison.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Eiffel had a permit for the tower to stand for 20 years. It was to be dismantled in 1909, when its ownership would revert to the <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">City of Paris</a>. The City had planned to tear it down (part of the original contest rules for designing a tower was that it should be easy to dismantle) but as the tower proved to be valuable for communication purposes, it was allowed to remain after the expiry of the permit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Eiffel made use of his apartment at the top of the tower to carry out <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological observations</a>, and also used the tower to perform experiments on the action of air resistance on falling bodies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reichelt.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Franz Reichelt's preparations and jump from the Eiffel Tower</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">1900 <i>Exposition Universelle</i></a>, the lifts in the east and west legs were replaced by lifts running as far as the second level constructed by the French firm Fives-Lille. These had a compensating mechanism to keep the floor level as the angle of ascent changed at the first level, and were driven by a similar hydraulic mechanism to the Otis lifts, although this was situated at the base of the tower. Hydraulic pressure was provided by pressurised accumulators located near this mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-vogel_23-4_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogel_23-4-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time the lift in the north pillar was removed and replaced by a staircase to the first level. The layout of both first and second levels was modified, with the space available for visitors on the second level. The original lift in the south pillar was removed 13 years later.</p>
<p>On 19 October 1901, <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Santos-Dumont" title="Alberto Santos-Dumont">Alberto Santos-Dumont</a>, flying his <a href="/wiki/Santos-Dumont_number_6" title="Santos-Dumont number 6">No.6</a> <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a>, won a 100,000-franc prize offered by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Deutsch_de_la_Meurthe" title="Henri Deutsch de la Meurthe">Henri Deutsch de la Meurthe</a> for the first person to make a flight from <a href="/wiki/Saint-Cloud" title="Saint-Cloud">St. Cloud</a> to the Eiffel Tower and back in less than half an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many innovations took place at the Eiffel Tower in the early 20th century. In 1910, <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">Father</a> <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Wulf" title="Theodor Wulf">Theodor Wulf</a> measured <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a> at the top and bottom of the tower. He found more at the top than expected, incidentally discovering what are known today as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wulf_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wulf-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Just two years later, on 4 February 1912, Austrian tailor <a href="/wiki/Franz_Reichelt" title="Franz Reichelt">Franz Reichelt</a> died after jumping from the first level of the tower (a height of 57 metres) to demonstrate his <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachute</a> design.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1914, at the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War&#160;I</a>, a radio transmitter located in the tower <a href="/wiki/Radio_jamming" title="Radio jamming">jammed</a> German radio communications, seriously hindering their advance on Paris and contributing to the Allied victory at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="First Battle of the Marne">First Battle of the Marne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1925 to 1934, illuminated signs for <a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn" title="Citroën">Citroën</a> adorned three of the tower's sides, making it the tallest advertising space in the world at the time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In April 1935, the tower was used to make experimental low-resolution <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> transmissions, using a <a href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave" class="mw-redirect">shortwave</a> transmitter of 200 watts power. On 17 November, an improved 180-line transmitter was installed.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On two separate but related occasions in 1925, the con artist <a href="/wiki/Victor_Lustig" title="Victor Lustig">Victor Lustig</a> "sold" the tower for scrap metal.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> A year later, in February 1926, pilot Leon Collet was killed trying to fly under the tower. His aircraft became entangled in an aerial belonging to a wireless station.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> On 2 May 1929, a <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">bust</a> of Gustave Eiffel by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Bourdelle" title="Antoine Bourdelle">Antoine Bourdelle</a> was unveiled at the base of the north leg.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1930, the tower lost the title of the world's tallest structure when the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> was completed.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1938, the decorative arcade around the first level was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg/170px-American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg/255px-American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg/340px-American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2320" data-file-height="2947" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_soldiers_watch_as_the_Tricolor_flies_from_the_Eiffel_Tower_again.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American soldiers watch the French flag flying on the Eiffel Tower, c. 25 August 1944</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon the <a href="/wiki/Military_Administration_in_France_(Nazi_Germany)" title="Military Administration in France (Nazi Germany)" class="mw-redirect">German occupation</a> of Paris in 1940, the lift cables were <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">cut</a> by the French. The tower was closed to the public during the occupation and the lifts were not repaired until 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-Harriss_80_4_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harriss_80_4-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1940, German soldiers had to climb the tower to hoist the <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a>, but the flag was so large it blew away just a few hours later, and was replaced by a smaller one.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When visiting Paris, Hitler chose to stay on the ground. In August 1944, when the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> were nearing Paris, Hitler ordered General <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_von_Choltitz" title="Dietrich von Choltitz">Dietrich von Choltitz</a>, the military governor of Paris, to demolish the tower along with the rest of the city. Von Choltitz disobeyed the order.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> On 25 June, before the Germans had been driven out of Paris, the Nazi flag was replaced with a Tricolour by two men from the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_national_de_la_Marine" title="Musée national de la Marine">French Naval Museum</a>, who narrowly beat three men led by Lucien Sarniguet, who had lowered the Tricolour on 13 June 1940 when Paris fell to the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-Harriss_80_4_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harriss_80_4-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 January 1956, a fire started in the television transmitter, damaging the top of the tower. Repairs took a year, and in 1957, the present radio aerial was added to the top.<sup id="cite_ref-events_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-events-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1964, the Eiffel Tower was officially declared to be a historical monument by the Minister of Cultural Affairs, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux" title="André Malraux">André Malraux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> A year later, due to increasing visitor numbers, an additional lift system was installed in the north pillar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>According to interviews, in 1967, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> Mayor <a href="/wiki/Jean_Drapeau" title="Jean Drapeau">Jean Drapeau</a> negotiated a secret agreement with <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> for the tower to be dismantled and temporarily relocated to Montreal to serve as a landmark and tourist attraction during <a href="/wiki/Expo_67" title="Expo 67">Expo 67</a>. The plan was allegedly vetoed by the company operating the tower out of fear that the French government could refuse permission for the tower to be restored in its original location.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1982, the original lifts between the second and third levels were replaced after 97 years in service. These had been closed to the public between November and March because the water in the hydraulic drive tended to freeze. The new cars operate in pairs, with one counterbalancing the other, and perform the journey in one stage, reducing the journey time from eight minutes to less than two minutes. At the same time, two new emergency staircases were installed, replacing the original spiral staircases. In 1983, the south pillar was fitted with an electrically driven Otis lift to serve the <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne_(restaurant)" title="Jules Verne (restaurant)" class="mw-redirect">Jules Verne</a> restaurant.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Fives-Lille lifts in the east and west legs, fitted in 1899, were extensively refurbished in 1986. The cars were replaced, and a computer system was installed to completely automate the lifts. The <a href="/wiki/Motive_power" title="Motive power">motive power</a> was moved from the water hydraulic system to a new electrically driven oil-filled hydraulic system, and the original water hydraulics were retained solely as a counterbalance system.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> A service lift was added to the south pillar for moving small loads and maintenance personnel three years later.</p>
<p>On 31 March 1984, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Moriarty" title="Robert Moriarty" class="mw-redirect">Robert Moriarty</a> flew a <a href="/wiki/Beechcraft_Bonanza" title="Beechcraft Bonanza">Beechcraft Bonanza</a> under the tower.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1987, <a href="/wiki/A.J._Hackett" title="A.J. Hackett" class="mw-redirect">A.J. Hackett</a> made one of his first <a href="/wiki/Bungee_jumping" title="Bungee jumping">bungee jumps</a> from the top of the Eiffel Tower, using a special cord he had helped develop. Hackett was arrested by the police.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> On 27 October 1991, <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Devaux" title="Thierry Devaux">Thierry Devaux</a>, along with mountain guide Hervé Calvayrac, performed a series of acrobatic figures while bungee jumping from the second floor of the tower. Facing the Champ de Mars, Devaux used an electric winch between figures to go back up to the second floor. When firemen arrived, he stopped after the sixth jump.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 December 1999, for its "Countdown to the Year 2000" celebration, flashing lights and high-powered <a href="/wiki/Searchlight" title="Searchlight">searchlights</a> were installed on the tower. Fireworks were set off all over it. An exhibition above a cafeteria on the first floor commemorates this event. The searchlights on top of the tower made it a beacon in Paris's night sky, and 20,000 flashing bulbs gave the tower a sparkly appearance for five minutes every hour on the hour.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> On 31 December 2000, the lights sparkled blue for several nights to herald the new millennium. The sparkly lighting continued for 18 months until July 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-card11_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-card11-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The sparkling lights were turned on again on 21 June 2003, and the display was planned to continue for 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-card11_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-card11-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The tower received its 200,000,000th guest on 28 November 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2004, the Eiffel Tower began hosting a seasonal ice rink on the first level.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Glass_floor" title="Glass floor">glass floor</a> was installed on the first level during the 2014 refurbishment.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Material">Material</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg/220px-Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg/330px-Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg/440px-Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2935" data-file-height="2013" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sous_la_Tour_Eiffel_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Eiffel Tower from below</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddled iron</a> (wrought iron) of the Eiffel Tower weighs 7,300 tonnes, and the entire structure, including non-metal components, is approximately 10,000&#160;tonnes. As a demonstration of the economy of design, if the 7,300 tonnes of metal in the structure were melted down, it would fill the 125&#160;m<sup>2</sup> base to a depth of only 6.25&#160;cm (2.5&#160;in), assuming the density of the metal to be 7.8 tonnes per cubic metre.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, a cubic box surrounding the tower (324&#160;m x 125&#160;m x 125&#160;m) would contain 6,200&#160;tonnes of air, weighing almost as much as the iron itself. Depending on the ambient temperature, the top of the tower may shift away from the sun by up to 18&#160;cm (7.1&#160;in) due to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> of the metal on the side facing the sun.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_considerations">Wind considerations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg/170px-Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg/255px-Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg/340px-Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Base_of_Eiffel_Tower_at_Night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Looking upwards from the base of the tower at night</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the tower was built, many people were shocked by its daring form. Eiffel was accused of trying to create something artistic with no regard to the principles of engineering. However, Eiffel and his engineers, as experienced bridge builders, understood the importance of wind forces, and knew that if they were going to build the tallest structure in the world, they had to be sure it could withstand them. In an interview with the newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Temps_(Paris)" title="Le Temps (Paris)">Le Temps</a></i> published on 14 February 1887, Eiffel said,<sup id="cite_ref-debate_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debate-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Now to what phenomenon did I give primary concern in designing the Tower? It was <a href="/wiki/Wind_resistance" title="Wind resistance" class="mw-redirect">wind resistance</a>. Well then! I hold that the curvature of the monument's four outer edges, which is as mathematical calculation dictated it should be [...] will give a great impression of strength and beauty, for it will reveal to the eyes of the observer the boldness of the design as a whole.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Eiffel used empirical and graphical methods to account for the effects of wind rather than a specific mathematical formula. Careful examination of the tower reveals a basically exponential shape (actually two different exponentials, the lower section being over-designed to ensure resistance to wind forces). Several mathematical explanations have been proposed over the years for the success of the design; the most recent is described as a non-linear integral equation based on counteracting the wind pressure on any point on the tower with the tension between the construction elements at that point.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedaily2005_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily2005-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The tower sways by only 6–7&#160;cm (2–3&#160;in) in the wind.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accommodation">Accommodation</span></h3>
<p>When originally built, the first level contained three restaurants—one French, one <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Flemish_Region" title="Flemish Region">Flemish</a>—and an "Anglo-American Bar". After the exposition closed, the Flemish restaurant was converted to a 250-seat theatre. A promenade 2.6-metre (8&#160;ft 6&#160;in) wide ran around the outside of the first level. At the top, there were laboratories for various experiments, and a small apartment reserved for Gustave Eiffel to entertain guests, which is now open to the public, complete with period decorations and lifelike <a href="/wiki/Mannequin" title="Mannequin">mannequins</a> of Eiffel and some of his notable guests.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger_lifts">Passenger lifts</span></h3>
<p>The arrangement of the lifts has been changed several times during the tower's history. Given the elasticity of the cables and the time taken to align the cars with the landings, each lift, in normal service, takes an average of 8 minutes and 50 seconds to do the round trip, spending an average of 1 minute and 15 seconds at each level. The average journey time between levels is 1 minute. The original hydraulic mechanism is on public display in a small museum at the base of the east and west legs. Because the mechanism requires frequent lubrication and maintenance, public access is often restricted. The rope mechanism of the north tower can be seen as visitors exit the lift.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engraved_names">Engraved names</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_72_names_on_the_Eiffel_Tower" title="List of the 72 names on the Eiffel Tower">List of the 72 names on the Eiffel Tower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg/220px-Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg/330px-Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg/440px-Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3840" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eiffel_Tower_details.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Names engraved on the tower</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gustave Eiffel engraved on the tower the names of 72 French scientists, engineers and mathematicians in recognition of their contributions to the building of the tower. Eiffel chose this "invocation of science" because of his concern over the artists' protest. At the beginning of the 20th century, the engravings were painted over, but they were restored in 1986–87 by the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Société Nouvelle d'exploitation de la Tour Eiffel</span>, a company operating the tower.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aesthetics">Aesthetics</span></h3>
<p>The tower was originally painted in three shades: lighter at the top, getting progressively darker towards the bottom to perfectly complement the Parisian sky.<sup id="cite_ref-paintingcolor_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paintingcolor-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The colour is periodically changed; as of 2013 the tower is bronze coloured.<sup id="cite_ref-painting_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-painting-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> On the first level are interactive consoles hosting a poll for the colour to use for the next repaint.</p>
<p>The only non-structural elements are the four decorative grill-work arches, added in Sauvestre's sketches, which served to make the tower look more substantial and to make a more impressive entrance to the exposition.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One of the great Hollywood movie clichés is that the view from a Parisian window always includes the tower. In reality, since zoning restrictions limit the height of most buildings in Paris to seven storeys, only a small number of tall buildings have a clear view of the tower.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance">Maintenance</span></h3>
<p>Maintenance of the tower includes applying 50 to 60 tonnes (49 to 59 long tons; 55 to 66 short tons) of paint every seven years to prevent it from <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rusting</a>. The height of the Eiffel Tower varies by up to 15&#160;cm (5.9&#160;in) due to thermal expansion.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg/220px-Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg.png" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg/330px-Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg/440px-Eiffel_Tower_Visitors.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="772" data-file-height="345" /></a>

Visitors queuing to enter the tower</div>

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3>
<p>The nearest <a href="/wiki/Paris_M%C3%A9tro" title="Paris Métro">Paris Métro</a> station is <a href="/wiki/Bir-Hakeim_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Bir-Hakeim (Paris Métro)">Bir-Hakeim</a> and the nearest <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9seau_Express_R%C3%A9gional" title="Réseau Express Régional">RER</a> station is Champ de Mars-Tour Eiffel.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The tower itself is located at the intersection of the quai Branly and the Pont d'Iéna.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span></h3>
<p>More than 250&#160;million people have visited the tower since it was completed in 1889: in 2012 there were 6,180,000 visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-Key_figures_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Key_figures-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The tower is the most-visited paid monument in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> An average of 25,000 people ascend the tower every day which can result in long queues.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Tickets can be purchased online to avoid the long queues.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurants">Restaurants</span></h3>
<p>The tower has two restaurants: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Le 58 Tour Eiffel</span> on the first level, and <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Le Jules Verne</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Gourmet" title="Gourmet">gourmet</a> restaurant with its own lift on the second level. This restaurant has one star in the <a href="/wiki/Michelin_Guide" title="Michelin Guide">Michelin Red Guide</a>. It is run by the multi-<a href="/wiki/Michelin_star" title="Michelin star" class="mw-redirect">Michelin star</a> chef <a href="/wiki/Alain_Ducasse" title="Alain Ducasse">Alain Ducasse</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and owes its name to the famous science-fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Replicas">Replicas</span></h2>

<div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Eiffel_Tower_replicas" title="List of Eiffel Tower replicas">List of Eiffel Tower replicas</a></div>
<p>As one of the most iconic landmarks in the world, the Eiffel Tower has been the inspiration for the creation of at least 12 replicas of a quarter scale or larger, and there are more than 40 duplicates and similar towers of various scales around the world. An early example is the <a href="/wiki/Blackpool_Tower" title="Blackpool Tower">Blackpool Tower</a> in England. The mayor of Blackpool, Sir John Bickerstaffe, was so impressed on seeing the Eiffel Tower at the 1889 exposition that he commissioned a similar tower to be built in his town.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Two full size replicas exist: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Tower" title="Tokyo Tower">Tokyo Tower</a> in Japan and the <a href="/wiki/Long_Ta" title="Long Ta">Long Ta</a> communications tower in northern China.</p>
<p>In 2011, the American TV show <i>Pricing the Priceless</i> speculated that the Eiffel Tower would cost $480&#160;million to build, that the land on which the tower stands is worth $350&#160;million, and that the scrap value of the tower is $3.5&#160;million. The show estimated that the tower makes a profit of $29&#160;million per year, but it is unlikely the Eiffel Tower is managed so as to maximize profit.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span></h2>
<p>The tower has been used for making radio transmissions since the beginning of the 20th century. Until the 1950s, sets of aerial wires ran from the <a href="/wiki/Cupola" title="Cupola">cupola</a> to anchors on the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Avenue_de_Suffren&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Avenue de Suffren (page does not exist)">Avenue de Suffren</a> and Champ de Mars. These were connected to <a href="/wiki/Longwave" title="Longwave">longwave</a> transmitters in small bunkers. In 1909, a permanent underground radio centre was built near the south pillar, which still exists today. On 20 November 1913, the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Observatory" title="Paris Observatory">Paris Observatory</a>, using the Eiffel Tower as an aerial, exchanged wireless signals with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">United States Naval Observatory</a>, which used an aerial in <a href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia" class="mw-redirect">Arlington</a>, Virginia. The object of the transmissions was to measure the difference in longitude between Paris and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, radio and television signals are transmitted from the Eiffel Tower.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Image_copyright_claims">Image copyright claims</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_(pixinn.net).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/220px-TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/330px-TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/440px-TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1709" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TourEiffel_BleuBlancRouge_(pixinn.net).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Eiffel Tower illuminated in the colors of the French flag.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The tower and its representations have long been in the <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a>. In June 1990, however, a French court ruled that a special lighting display on the tower in 1989 to mark the tower's 100th anniversary was an "original visual creation" protected by copyright. The <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Cassation_(France)" title="Court of Cassation (France)">Court of Cassation</a>, France's judicial court of last resort, upheld the ruling in March 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Société d'Exploitation de la Tour Eiffel</span> (SETE) now considers any illumination of the tower to be under copyright.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, it is illegal to publish contemporary photographs of the tower at night without permission in France and some other countries.</p>
<p>The imposition of copyright has been controversial. The Director of Documentation for what was then called the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Société Nouvelle d'exploitation de la Tour Eiffel</span> (SNTE), Stéphane Dieu, commented in January 2005, "It is really just a way to manage commercial use of the image, so that it isn't used in ways we don't approve." However, it could also be used to prohibit the publication of tourist photographs of the tower at night,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as well as hindering non-profit and semi-commercial publication of images of the tower. French doctrine and jurisprudence traditionally allows pictures incorporating a copyrighted work as long as their presence is incidental or accessory to the subject being represented,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> a reasoning akin to the <i><a href="/wiki/De_minimis" title="De minimis">de minimis</a></i> rule. Therefore, SETE could not claim copyright on photographs of Paris which happen to include the lit tower.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower_in_popular_culture" title="Eiffel Tower in popular culture">Eiffel Tower in popular culture</a></div>
<p>As a global landmark, the Eiffel Tower is featured in films, video games and TV shows.</p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Commitment_ceremony" title="Commitment ceremony" class="mw-redirect">commitment ceremony</a> in 2007, <a href="/wiki/Erika_Eiffel" title="Erika Eiffel">Erika Eiffel</a>, an American woman, "married" the Eiffel Tower. Her relationship with the tower has been the subject of extensive global publicity.<sup id="cite_ref-GlobeAndMail2009_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobeAndMail2009-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taller_structures">Taller structures</span></h2>
<p>Although it was the world's tallest structure when completed in 1889, the Eiffel Tower has since lost its standing both as the tallest lattice tower and as the tallest structure in France.</p>
